             </div> </td>

         <p>The <b>cucumber</b> (<i>Cucumis sativus</i>) is a widely-cultivated <a href="/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants" title="Vine">creeping vine</a> plant in the family <a href="/wiki/Cucurbitaceae" title="Cucurbitaceae">Cucurbitaceae</a> that bears cylindrical to spherical <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>, which are used as <a href="/wiki/Culinary_vegetable" class="mw-redirect" title="Culinary vegetable">culinary vegetables</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> Considered an annual plant,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> there are three main types of cucumber—slicing, <a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber" title="Pickled cucumber">pickling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seedless_fruit" title="Seedless fruit">seedless</a>—within which several <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> have been created. The cucumber originates from Himalaya to China (Yunnan, Guizhou, Guangxi) and N. Thailand,<sup id="cite_ref-powo_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-powo-3">[3]</a></sup> but now grows on most <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, and many different types of cucumber are grown commercially and traded on the <a href="/wiki/Global_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Global market">global market</a>. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Wild_cucumber" title="Wild cucumber">wild cucumber</a></i> refers to plants in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Echinocystis" title="Echinocystis">Echinocystis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Marah_(plant)" title="Marah (plant)">Marah</a></i>, though the two are not closely related. </p> 

         <meta property="mw:PageProp/toc"> 

         <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2> 

         <p>The cucumber is a <a href="/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants" title="Vine">creeping vine</a> that roots in the ground and grows up <a href="/wiki/Trellis_(agriculture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trellis (agriculture)">trellises</a> or other supporting frames, wrapping around supports with thin, spiraling <a href="/wiki/Tendrils" class="mw-redirect" title="Tendrils">tendrils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The plant may also root in a <a href="/wiki/Soilless_gardening" class="mw-redirect" title="Soilless gardening">soilless medium</a>, whereby it will sprawl along the ground in lieu of a supporting structure. The vine has large leaves that form a <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a> over the fruits.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>

         <p>The fruit of typical cultivars of cucumber is roughly <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylindrical</a>, but elongated with tapered ends, and may be as large as 62 centimeters (24 in) long and 10 centimeters (4 in) in diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-ZhangLi2019_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZhangLi2019-5">[5]</a></sup> </p>

         <p>Cucumber fruits consist of 95% water (see nutrition table). In <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botanical</a> terms, the cucumber is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Pepo_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepo (botany)"><i>pepo</i></a>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">botanical berry</a> with a hard outer rind and no internal divisions. However, much like <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squashes</a>, it is often perceived, prepared, and eaten as a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowering_and_pollination">Flowering and pollination</span></h3> 

         <div class="thumb tleft">

          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">

         <p>Most cucumber cultivars are seeded and require pollination. For this purpose, thousands of <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey</a> <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">beehives</a> are annually carried to cucumber fields just before bloom. Cucumbers may also be pollinated via <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebees</a> and several other bee species. Most cucumbers that require pollination are <a href="/wiki/Self_incompatibility_in_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Self incompatibility in plants">self-incompatible</a>, thus requiring the <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> of another plant in order to form <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> and fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-Nonnecke_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nonnecke-7">[7]</a></sup> Some self-compatible cultivars exist that are related to the '<a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">Lemon</a>' cultivar.<sup id="cite_ref-Nonnecke_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nonnecke-7">[7]</a></sup> </p>

         <p>A few <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> of cucumber are <a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpy" title="Parthenocarpy">parthenocarpic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Blossom" title="Blossom">blossoms</a> of which create <a href="/wiki/Seedless_fruit" title="Seedless fruit">seedless fruit</a> without <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>, which degrades the eating quality of these cultivar. In the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_United_States" title="Agriculture in the United States">United States</a>, these are usually grown in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a> are excluded. In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, they are grown outdoors in some regions, where bees are likewise excluded.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>

         <p>Traditional cultivars produce male blossoms first, then female, in about equivalent numbers. Newer <a href="/wiki/Gynoecious" class="mw-redirect" title="Gynoecious">gynoecious</a> hybrid cultivars produce almost all female blossoms. They may have a <a href="/wiki/Pollenizer" title="Pollenizer">pollenizer</a> cultivar interplanted, and the number of beehives per unit area is increased, but temperature changes induce male flowers even on these plants, which may be sufficient for pollination to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Nonnecke_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nonnecke-7">[7]</a></sup> </p>

         <p>In 2009, an international team of researchers announced they had sequenced the cucumber <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>

            <td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data">

             <hr>

             <div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;">

              <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/fdc-app.html#/food-details/168409/nutrients">Link to USDA database entry</a>

             </div></td>

           </tr>

           <tr class="plainlist">

            <td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data">

             <div style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.15em;line-height:1.25em;"> 

              <ul>

               <li>Units</li> 

               <li>μg = <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> • mg = <a href="/wiki/Milligram" class="mw-redirect" title="Milligram">milligrams</a></li> 

               <li>IU = <a href="/wiki/International_unit" title="International unit">International units</a></li>

              </ul> 

             </div></td>

           </tr>

           <tr>

            <td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;"><sup>†</sup>Percentages are roughly approximated using <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">US recommendations</a> for adults.</td>

           </tr>

          </tbody>

         </table> 

         <p>Raw cucumber (with <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">peel</a>) is 95% water, 4% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. A <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>100-gram (<span class="frac" role="math">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span>-ounce) <a href="/wiki/Serving_size" title="Serving size">reference serving</a> provides 65 kilojoules (16 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>. It has a low content of <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a>: it is notable only for <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>, at 16% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (table). </p>

         <p>Depending on variety, cucumbers may have a mild <a href="/wiki/Melon" title="Melon">melon</a> aroma and flavor, in part resulting from unsaturated <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>, such as <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal">(E,Z)-nona-2,6-dienal</a></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cis%E2%80%93trans_isomerism" title="Cis–trans isomerism"><i>cis</i>- and <i>trans</i>-</a> <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a> of <a href="/wiki/2-Nonenal" title="2-Nonenal">2-nonenal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The slightly <a href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitter (taste)">bitter</a> taste of cucumber rind results from <a href="/wiki/Cucurbitacins" class="mw-redirect" title="Cucurbitacins">cucurbitacins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h2> 

         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

          See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cucumber_varieties" title="List of cucumber varieties">List of cucumber varieties</a>

         </div> 

         <p>In general <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivation</a>, cucumbers are classified into three main <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> groups: slicing, <a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber" title="Pickled cucumber">pickled</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seedless_fruit" title="Seedless fruit">seedless/burpless</a>. </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slicing">Slicing</span></h3> 

         <p>Cucumbers grown to eat fresh are called <i>slicing cucumbers</i>. The main varieties of slicers mature on <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vines</a> with large leaves that provide shading.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>

         <p>Slicers grown commercially for the North American market are generally longer, smoother, more uniform in color, and have much tougher skin. In contrast, those in other countries, often called <a href="/wiki/European_cucumber" title="European cucumber"><b>European cucumbers</b></a>, are smaller and have thinner, more delicate skin, often with fewer seeds, thus are often being sold in plastic skin for protection. This variety may also be called a <b>telegraph cucumber</b>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pickling">Pickling</span></h3> 

         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

          Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber" title="Pickled cucumber">Pickled cucumber</a>

         </div> 

         <p><a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber" title="Pickled cucumber">Pickling</a> with <a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Brined_pickles" title="Pickled cucumber">brine</a>, sugar, <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a>, and spices creates various flavored products from cucumbers and other foods.<sup id="cite_ref-avi_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avi-13">[13]</a></sup> Although any cucumber can be pickled, commercial pickles are made from cucumbers specially bred for uniformity of length-to-diameter ratio and lack of voids in the flesh. Those cucumbers intended for pickling, called <b>picklers</b>, grow to about 7 to 10 cm (3 to 4 in) long and 2.5 cm (1 in) wide. Compared to slicers, picklers tend to be shorter, thicker, less-regularly shaped, and have bumpy skin with tiny white or black-dotted spines. Color can vary from creamy yellow to pale or dark green.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 

         <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gherkin">Gherkin</span></h4> 

         <p><a href="/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Gherkin" title="Pickled cucumber">Gherkins</a>, also called <b>cornichons</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-kitchn_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kitchn-14">[14]</a></sup> or <b>baby pickles</b>, are small cucumbers, typically those 2.5 to 12.5 centimetres (1 to 5 in) in length, often with bumpy skin, which are typically used for pickling.<sup id="cite_ref-zon_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zon-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wifss_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wifss-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-india_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-india-17">[17]</a></sup> The word <i>gherkin</i> comes from the early modern <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>gurken</i> or <i>augurken</i> ('small pickled cucumber').<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The term is also used in the name for <i><a href="/wiki/Cucumis_anguria" title="Cucumis anguria">Cucumis anguria</a></i>, the <i>West Indian gherkin</i>, a closely related species.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burpless">Burpless</span></h3> 

         <p>Burpless cucumbers are sweeter and have a thinner skin than other varieties of cucumber. They are reputed to be easy to digest and to have a pleasant taste. They can grow as long as 60 centimeters (2 ft), are nearly seedless, and have a delicate skin. Most commonly grown in greenhouses, these <a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" class="mw-redirect" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> cucumbers are often found in <a href="/wiki/Grocery_store" title="Grocery store">grocery markets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrink_wrap" title="Shrink wrap">shrink-wrapped</a> in plastic. They are marketed as either burpless or seedless, as the seeds and skin of other varieties of cucumbers are said to give some people gas.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>

         <p>In 2020, world production of cucumbers and gherkins was 91 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China with 80% of the total.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-21">[21]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation_history">Cultivation history</span></h2> 

         <p>Cultivated for at least 3,000 years, the cucumber originated from <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_India" title="Agriculture in India">India</a>, where a great many varieties have been observed, along with its closest living relative, <i><a href="/wiki/Cucumis_hystrix" title="Cucumis hystrix">Cucumis hystrix</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It was probably introduced to Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece" title="Agriculture in ancient Greece">Greeks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Rome" title="Agriculture in ancient Rome">Romans</a>. Records of cucumber cultivation appear in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> in the 9th century, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_England" title="Agriculture in England">England</a> in the 14th century, and in North America by the mid-16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Renner_2007_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renner_2007-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doijode_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doijode-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Empire">Roman Empire</span></h3> 

         <p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> had the cucumber on his table daily during summer and winter. In order to have it available for his table every day of the year, the Romans reportedly used artificial methods of growing (similar to the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouse system</a>), whereby <i>mirrorstone</i> refers to Pliny's <i>lapis specularis</i>, believed to have been sheet <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-AncientInventions_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AncientInventions-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 

         <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>

         <blockquote class="templatequote">

          <p>Indeed, he was never without it; for he had raised beds made in frames upon wheels, by means of which the cucumbers were moved and exposed to the full heat of the sun; while, in winter, they were withdrawn, and placed under the protection of frames glazed with mirrorstone.</p>

          <div class="templatequotecite">

           — <cite>Pliny the Elder, <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i> XIX.xxiii, "Vegetables of a Cartilaginous Nature—Cucumbers. Pepones"</cite>

          </div>

         </blockquote> 

         <p>Reportedly, they were also cultivated in <i>specularia</i>, cucumber houses glazed with oiled cloth.<sup id="cite_ref-AncientInventions_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AncientInventions-26">[26]</a></sup> Pliny describes the Italian fruit as very small, probably like a <a href="/wiki/Gherkin" class="mw-redirect" title="Gherkin">gherkin</a>. He also describes the preparation of a medication known as <i>elaterium</i>. However, some scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2013)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe that he was instead referring to <i><a href="/wiki/Ecballium_elaterium" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecballium elaterium">Ecballium elaterium</a></i>, known in pre-<a href="/wiki/Linnean_nomenclature" class="mw-redirect" title="Linnean nomenclature">Linnean</a> times as <i>Cucumis silvestris</i> or <i>Cucumis asininus</i> ('wild cucumber' or 'donkey cucumber'), a species different from the common cucumber.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Pliny also writes about several other varieties of cucumber, including the cultivated cucumber,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and remedies from the different types (9 from the cultivated; 5 from the "anguine;" and 26 from the "wild"). </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3> 

         <p><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> had cucumbers grown in his gardens in the 8th/9th century. They were reportedly introduced into England in the early 14th century, lost, then reintroduced approximately 250 years later. The <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a> (through the <a href="/wiki/Italian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian people">Italian</a> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>) brought cucumbers to <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> in 1494. In 1535, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Cartier" title="Jacques Cartier">Jacques Cartier</a>, a French explorer, found "very great cucumbers" grown on the site of what is now <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early-modern_age">Early-modern age</span></h3> 

         <div class="thumb tright">

          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">

           <a href="/wiki/File:Trans-2,cis-6-Nonadienal.png" class="image"><img alt="trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal, or cucumber aldehyde" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png/250px-Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png/375px-Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png/500px-Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png 2x" data-file-width="2175" data-file-height="689"></a> 

           <div class="thumbcaption">

            <div class="magnify">

             <a href="/wiki/File:Trans-2,cis-6-Nonadienal.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>

            </div><a href="/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal" title="Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal">Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal</a>, or <i>cucumber aldehyde</i>, is a component of the distinctive aroma of cucumbers.

           </div>

          </div>

         </div> 

         <p>Throughout the 16th century, European trappers, traders, <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> hunters, and explorers bartered for the products of American Indian <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. The tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> learned from the Spanish how to grow European crops. The farmers on the Great Plains included the <a href="/wiki/Mandan" title="Mandan">Mandan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abenaki" title="Abenaki">Abenaki</a>. They obtained cucumbers and <a href="/wiki/Watermelon" title="Watermelon">watermelons</a> from the Spanish, and added them to the crops they were already growing, including several varieties of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin" title="Pumpkin">pumpkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Squash (fruit)">squash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gourd" title="Gourd">gourd</a> plants.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> were also growing them when the first Europeans visited them.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>

         <p> In 1630, the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Francis_Higginson" title="Francis Higginson">Francis Higginson</a> produced a book called <i>New-Englands Plantation</i> in which, describing a garden on Conant's Island in <a href="/wiki/Boston_Harbor" title="Boston Harbor">Boston Harbor</a> known as <i>The Governor's Garden</i>, he states:<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

         <blockquote>

          <p>The countrie aboundeth naturally with store of roots of great varietie [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] and good to eat. Our turnips, parsnips, and carrots are here both bigger and sweeter than is ordinary to be found in England. Here are store of pompions, cowcumbers, and other things of that nature which I know not…</p>

         </blockquote>

         <p>In <i>New England Prospect</i> (1633, England), William Wood published observations he made in 1629 in America:<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>

         <blockquote>

          <p>The ground affords very good kitchin gardens, for Turneps, Parsnips, Carrots, Radishes, and Pompions, Muskmillons, Isquoter-squashes, coucumbars, Onyons, and whatever grows well in England grows as well there, many things being better and larger. [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]</p>

         </blockquote> 

         <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Enlightenment_and_later">Age of Enlightenment and later</span></h3> 

         <div class="thumb tright">

          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

           <a href="/wiki/File:Lobster,_Crab,_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg/220px-Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg/330px-Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg/440px-Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7375" data-file-height="4124"></a> 

           <div class="thumbcaption">

            <div class="magnify">

             <a href="/wiki/File:Lobster,_Crab,_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>

            </div><i>Lobster, Crab, and a Cucumber</i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Hunt_(painter)" title="William Henry Hunt (painter)">William Henry Hunt</a> (watercolour, 1826 or 1827)

           </div>

          </div>

         </div> 

         <p>In the later 17th century, a prejudice developed against uncooked vegetables and fruits. A number of articles in contemporary health publications stated that uncooked plants brought on summer diseases and should be forbidden to children. The cucumber kept this reputation for an inordinate period of time, "fit only for consumption by cows," which some believe is why it gained the name, <i>cowcumber</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 

         <p><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a> wrote in his diary on 22 August 1663:<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>

         <blockquote>

          <p>[T]his day Sir W. Batten tells me that Mr. Newburne is dead of eating cowcumbers, of which the other day I heard of another, I think.</p>

         </blockquote> 

         <p>A copper <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a> made by Maddalena Bouchard between 1772 and 1793 shows this plant to have smaller, almost bean-shaped fruits, and small yellow flowers. The small form of the cucumber is figured in <a href="/wiki/Herbal" title="Herbal">Herbals</a> of the 16th century, however stating that "[i]f hung in a tube while in blossom, the Cucumber will grow to a most surprising length."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
